\documentclass[]{article} \usepackage[a4paper, portrait, margin=2cm]{geometry} \usepackage{graphicx} \graphicspath{{figures_podsumowanie/}} \usepackage[dvipsnames]{xcolor} \usepackage[T1]{fontenc} \usepackage{hyperref} \hypersetup{colorlinks, citecolor=blue, filecolor=blue, linkcolor=blue, urlcolor=blue} \usepackage{booktabs} \usepackage{enumitem} \usepackage{palatino} \usepackage[font=small,labelfont=bf]{caption} \usepackage{multicol} \setlength{\columnsep}{1.5em} \setlength{\columnseprule}{0mm} \usepackage{tikz} \usetikzlibrary{shapes,arrows} \newcommand{\compresslist}{ \setlength{\itemsep}{1pt} \setlength{\parskip}{0pt} \setlength{\parsep}{0pt} } \definecolor{lightblue}{rgb}{0.145,0.6666,1} \begin{document} \newcommand{\annot}[1]{\textbf{\textcolor{OrangeRed}{{{#1}}}}} \newcommand{\reply}[1]{\textbf{\color{Green} #1}} \newcommand{\todo}[1]{\textbf{\textcolor{Red}{{TODO: {#1}-}}}} \section{Current status} \subsection{Why $L_{IR}$ is at the same level in high-z sample?} \begin{figure}[h] \centering \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Regr_low_Acc_Lir.png} \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Regr_high_Acc_Lir.png} \caption{Correlations between $L_{IR}$ and accretion rate in low-z and high-z samples.} \label{fig:LIRvsAccrate} \end{figure} The infrared luminosity in the low-z sample correlates with the accretion rate/Eddington ratio. Potentially $L_{IR}$ is dominated by the torus dust emission and we see that it scales up with the accretion rate at low-z. However the situation looks different on high redshift --- $L_{IR}$ is constant across accretion rates. It is illustrated on Fig.~\ref{fig:LIRvsAccrate}. \begin{figure}[h] \centering \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{LirvsLeddvsBH_old_wise.png} \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Lirvsacc.png} \caption{The relation $\log_{10}L_{ir}$ vs $\log_{10} T_{acc}$, with color the $M_{BH}$ are marked for low-z (purple), high-z (green). Only the sub-sample with $WISE_{SNR}>3$ is shown. Low-z and high-z are clearly separated, with low-z having lower $L_{ir}$. } \label{fig:my_label} \end{figure} To get some clue we check slopes in the IR part and opt-UV part of the SEDs to find out whether some peculiarity occurs. As $L_{IR}$ and $L_{bol}$ are computed from the integral of a power law function fitted between two points, we want to be sure if gives accurate results. $L_{bol}$ is assumed to be dominated by the accretion disc emission. The illustration of this test is on Fig.~\ref{fig:Lslopes}. \begin{figure}[h] \centering \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Mean_SED.png} \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Mean_SED_high.png} \caption{Left panel: Mean SED of the low-z sample. Integrating SED IR region to obtain $L_{IR}$ (red shaded area) and opt-UV region for $L_{bol}$ (blue shaded area). Right panel: Mean SED of the high-z sample. \annot{It seems that high-z SED is much more influenced on the edges.}} \label{fig:meansed} \end{figure} \begin{figure}[h] \centering \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Power_Lir_L_bol.png} \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Power_Lir_L_bol_power_Lir.png} \caption{Slope in the log space for infrared and disc bolometric luminosity. Left panel: Colors of the markers code accretion rate in low-z and high-z samples. Only good quality data from WISE. Right panel: Color coding IR luminosity, no WISE quality cut.} \label{fig:Lslopes} \end{figure} We see that slopes in the high-z sample are more steep. Does it mean that we have strong FIR emission from a prominent starburst component, thus IR part is not dominated by the torus or is noticeably contaminated? To check that we would like to investigate Herschel data if available. \reply{We have only few high-z Herschel observations, but a few times more Spitzer MIPS 24 um observations. So far in majority of those few Herschel observed sources there is no strong starburst. In those Spitzer flux measurements usually we have lower value than in the WISE W4 filter. This fact together with impression given by the mean SEDs from Fig.~\ref{fig:meansed} may suggest that W4 led to overestimated $L_{IR}$ in the high-z sample.} To investigate this issue further we want to check \todo{W4-W3 plots} and eventually \todo{replace W4 and check W4 and MIPS 24 difference in small sample}. To check whether in the specific redshift range filters are affected by strong spectral features (like silicates) \todo{we may plot W4-W3 as a function of redshift}. \begin{figure}[h] \centering \includegraphics[width=1.\linewidth]{Lir_Lbol_lir_power.png} \caption{$\log_{10}$ L$_{IR}$ vs $\log_{10}$ L$_{BOL}$ with color the power index used for the integration for the L$_{IR}$. With the orange, circles the low-z are marked, and with the green, diamonds-like shapes the high-z quasars are marked.} \label{fig:Lir_Lbol_lir_power} \end{figure} \todo{use Spitzer MIPS instead of W4 and check LIR} \reply{New integrating scheme gives more accurate luminosities. The values of high-z luminosities are far more accurate.} \todo{updated figures - new luminosities} \annot{Now luminous high-z part is continuation of low-z trend. This is consistent with what we had with DR9 data before moving to DR16. The only issue remains is with lower luminosity high-z part, where L$_{IR}$ can be overestimated by W4 filter. Alternatively we have problem in UV with potential extinction which decreases apparent luminosity. On high-z is it possible to verify that? Check slope/L$_{BOL}$ power in comparison with the total distribution --- is it different? Or maybe there is a way to diagnose extinction with emission lines like it is the case with He + Na abs on low-z. But we would need a ratio of different hydrogen lines? } Stalevski suggest that disk radiation reprocessing dominates the torus emission. Although translating LIR/Lbol demands introducing corrections for low CF and high CF. But do we have additional mechanisms contributing in those branches. Additional radiation pressure shaping torus geometry? Accretion flow heating inside torus? Or disk luminosity/anisotropy acting different way for high CF/accretion rate? \subsection{CF evolution} \begin{figure}[h] \centering \includegraphics[width=1.\linewidth]{CFvsLeddvsBH_old.png} \caption{The relation between the $\log_{10}$ CF vs $\log_{10}$ accretion rate. High-z QSO have higher dispersion. By inferring relation of CF with accretion rate we are looking for the physical driver behind torus thickness. The CF have higher values for high-z, quasars with smaller $T_{acc}$, which means that more dust is present in high-z objects.} \label{fig:CFvsAccrate} \end{figure} On Fig.~\ref{fig:CFvsAccrate} we present relation between $\mathbf{CF = L_{IR}/L_{bol}}$ and accretion rate $\mathbf{\dot m = 1.62\ L_{bol}/L_{Edd}}$. It seems that the high-z sample, due to its constant-$L_{IR}$ issue, is driven by inverse dependence of CF on $L_{bol}$. While the low-z sample shows potentially realistic mild anti-correlation of CF with the accretion rate. Although we expected increasing thickness of torus with increasing $\dot m$ and we see positive correlation between $L_{IR}$ and $\dot m$ (Fig.~\ref{fig:LIRvsAccrate}), CF unexpectedly drops with $\dot m$ (Fig.~\ref{fig:CFvsAccratecor}). Does it really mean that the dust emission gets stronger with the increasing accretion rate, but the torus does not gets thicker? Is it possible to explain that with more extended torus with more heated dust along the radial direction although flattened by the strong continuum radiation which blows away upper parts of the torus? So CF would be driven by radiation pressure instead of gas pressure. Or the effect is again caused with some other kind of influence. On Fig.~\ref{fig:Lslopes} right panel the scatter is smaller for low-z and $L_{IR}$ power is less steep than in high-z. \begin{figure}[h] \centering \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Regr_low_Acc_CF.png} \includegraphics[width=0.49\linewidth]{Regr_high_Acc_CF.png} \caption{CF correlations with accretion rate: low-z and high-z cases.} \label{fig:CFvsAccratecor} \end{figure} Spodziewałem się dodatniej korelacji między tempem akrecji, a CF, co wskazywałoby na dominującą rolę ciśnienia gazu. Natomiast Mateusz pokazuje antykorelację - może to wskazywać na przewagę cisnienia promieniowania. Czyli: wraz z tempem akrecji zwiększa się jasność. Jasność przekładałaby się na ciśnienie promieniowania, które działa na ośrodek torusa. Większa jasność powodowałaby "rozwiewanie" torusa, a przez to mniejsze CF? Tylko mimo wątpliwości co do metod Raja, w porównaniu do jego pracy na oko widzę: - zgodność co do trendu średniego CF z redshiftem - rośnie, - przeciwny trend CF z MBH - maleje, - przeciwny trend CF z tempem akrecji - maleje. high-z steeper IR seen also in W1 vs SED L difference -> Viewing angle effect - is it important? Suh et al. 2019 - higher SFR and host masses on higher z, starburst component CF estimation have been done with use of X-ray data. \todo{references and conclusions}. \cite{2019ApJ...870...31I} \cite{2019ApJ...872....8M} \annot{we focus on high IR lum } \annot{do we see similar trend in low and high -z at high ir lum?} \annot{plot CF vs MMdot? } Test double cut: Lbol, LIR low-z and high-z --- different populations behave similarly in the overlapping region in the overlapping region (limiting Lbol and LIR from both sides) seems to be similar do the other params MBH, Mdot are similar as well? literature on polar dust literature on CF and evolution Toba, others? Ramos Padilla 2022 viewing angle from torus model SED fitting with cigale (skirtor, fritz), redshift 0-3.5 viewing angle of the torus, attenuation from the polar dust Polar dust dominates MIR in local AGN \cite{Asmus2016} Marchesi 2019 covering factor of nearby compton thick agn nustar from bat catalogue (borus) two fractions: cf~0.2, cf~1, generally drop in cf from 0.8 to 0.2 with x-ray luminosity Ichikawa 2019 nearby bat agn. Ramos Padilla et al. 2021 Fitting SEDs in the redshift range 0-3.5. They did not fully address polar dust but includes extinction by polar dust. They found that fitted E(B-V) from polar dust drops drastically above 0.2. Is it due to luminosity increase? Accretion power in their results is 2 orders of magnitude larger in Sy1 than in Sy2. Is it because underestimation of Sy2 BBB due to lack of intrinsic AGN extinction (apart from polar dust)? Or is it intrinsic effect that in Sy2 there are more objects with thicker torus? \todo{We may use this sample to check at least importance of stellar population for Lir derivation accuracy in our method. Or check our Lir - their Ltorus correlation or its ratio with the Lhost at IR, so check in what parameter range host contamination is not important. Also, check our Lbol - their LAGN correlation to test again our estimation accuracy. } \todo{On the other hand we have MBH from spectral catalogues.} Toba et al. 2021 Fitted SEDs with polar dust component (BB?), which contaminates warmer part of the torus ($\sim 5 \mu$m). Their definition of CF is a 4th-order polynomial of Lir/Lbol, but it is not total Lir but rather luminosity of the fitted component (torus or polar dust). Polar dust CF importance drops with the Lbol increase and even more with Edd. ratio (their Fig. 7), but not exactly with mass?! So the CF distribution along Lbol is dominated by MBH gradation? So Ramos Padilla et al. 2021 is right that among Sy2 low Lbol sources dominates due to thicker torus? Both Ramos and Toba have SDSS spectra, so we can check whether there is correlation with [O III] shift in broader components. Maybe stronger polar dust emission gives greater shifts? Or stronger broader components? \section{Previous checks} \subsection{najbliższe} Do przedyskutowania: \begin{itemize} \item SEDy ze Spitzerem i ich $L_{IR}$, \item uwspólnienie notatnika z obliczeniami w celu usprawnienia pracy? If the increasing (relative) infrared emission along EV1 is caused by an increasing torus covering factor, the resulting solid angle of the [O III] ionization cone will decrease along EV1, which will lead to a decreasing [O III] strength. However, the relative torus emission only increases by about 40\% from RFe II = 0 to RFe II = 2, while the av- erage [O III ] strength decreases by at least a factor of 5 over the same RFe II range (Figure 1). Therefore, a pure geometric effect (enhanced torus covering factor along EV1) cannot explain the observed EV1 cor- relation in terms of the [O III] strength — the change in the underlying quasar SED along EV1 is a more plausible explanation (see §5).
